BENTFLYVBJERG

Making Social Science Matter presents an exciting new approach to the
social and behavioral sciences. Instead o
---
 trying to emulate the natural
sciences and create a kind o
---
 general theory, Bent Flyvbjerg argues that
the strength o
---
 the social sciences lies in their rich, re
---
lexive analysis o
---

values and power — so essential to the social and economic development
o
---
 society. Moving beyond the purely analytic or technical, Flyvbjerg
compares the theoretical study o
---
 human activity with real-world situations and demonstrates how the social sciences can become relevant
again in the modern world. Power
---
ully argued, with clear methodological guidelines and practical examples, Making Social Science Matter
opens up a new 
---
uture 
---
or the social sciences, 
---
reed 
---
rom an inappropriate and misleading comparison with the natural sciences. Its empowering message will make it required reading 
---
or students and academics
across the social and behavioral sciences.
BENT FLYVBJERG is Pro
---
essor o
---
 Planning, Department o
---
 Development and Planning at Aalborg University, Denmark. He is the author o
---

numerous publications in twelve languages, most recently Rationality
and Power: Democracy in Practice (1998).

The Science Wars: a way out

Plato is dear to me, but dearer still is truth.

Aristotle

Physics envy and pre-Kantian shamans
When the May 1996 issue o
---
 the journal Social Text appeared, an issue
devoted to the understanding o
---
 “‘Science Wars,”’ the editors became
targets in these “‘wars”’ in ways they had not imagined. The issue included
a bogus article by New York University mathematical physicist Alan
Sokal, who 
---
eigned an earnest re
---
lection on the political and philosophical
implications o
---
 recent physics research 
---
or cultural studies.’ Sokal revealed the hoax himsel
---
, and it immediately became a hotly debated issue
in academic and popular media around the world.” The appearance o
---
 the
article was not only taken as a sign o
---
 shoddy scholarship by the Social
Text editors but as an exposé o
---
 cultural studies and social science in
general. For instance, Nobel prize-winning physicist Steven Weinberg
used the hoax to identi
---
y what he calls a 
---
undamental “‘opposition”’
between natural and social scientists, especially regarding what Weinberg
sees as dangerous anti-rationalism and relativism in social science and
cultural studies.’ Those on the other side o
---
 the “‘wars’’ countered by
criticizing Sokal and calling Weinberg and like-minded natural scientists
*‘pre-Kantian shaman|[s]”’ repeating the ‘‘mantras o
---
 particle physicists,”’
with their “reductionist view o
---
 science.’
The year be
---
ore Sokal’s hoax, the ‘‘wars’’ had raged over the scienti
---
ic
status o
---
 a high-pro
---
ile US National Opinion Research Center study,
which had been launched as a “‘de
---
initive survey” o
---
 sexual practices in
the United States.’ Here, too, doubts were raised not only about the
status o
---
 scholarship o
---
 the study in question, but o
---
 sociology and social
science as such. The study had received the doubt
---
ul honor o
---
 becoming
the topic o
---
 an editorial in The Economist under the heading “74.6% o
---

Sociology is Bunk.” In The New York Review o
---
 Books, Harvard biologist
and statistician R. C. Lewontin criticized the researchers behind the
I

2

Making social science matter

the survey questionstudy 
---
or believing what people said when 
---
illing in
Lewontin wrote, “‘to
naires on which the study builds. “‘It is 
---
rightening,’
who are so dea
---
 to
think that social science is in the hands o
---
 pro
---
essionals
elves [and
human nuance that they believe that people do not lie to thems
”’’ Lewontin
to others] about the most 
---
reighted aspects o
---
 their own lives.
nding to
concluded his review by warning social scientists that in prete
a kind o
---
 knowledge that it cannot

achieve, “‘social science can only

engender the scorn o
---
 natural scientists.”® Other social ‘science critics
participating in the debate talked o
---
 “dumbed-down”’ sociology and
social scientists’ “‘physics envy.”? The authors o
---
 the NORC study responded in kind by calling Lewontin’s review ‘‘pro
---
essionally incompetent” and motivated by an “‘evident animus against the social sciences in

general.”!° The authors also observed that the notion that an economist

or a sociologist should review work in population genetics, one o
---

Lewontin’s 
---
ields o
---
 competence, “would properly be greeted with de-

rision.’”!! While one might well agree with the latter point, the authors’

use o
---
 name-calling instead o
---
 substantive arguments in their attempt to
re
---
ute Lewontin’s criticism, leaves us wondering, not about the validity o
---

this criticism, but about what it is regarding natural and social science
that makes it 
---
airly common practice 
---
or natural scientists to review social
science, whereas the opposite is less common.
Good or bad?

However entertaining 
---
or bystanders, the mudslinging o
---
 the Science
Wars is unproductive. The Wars undoubtedly serve political and ideological purposes in the competition 
---
or research 
---
unds and in de
---
ining
what Charles Lindblom and Michel Foucault have called society’s “‘truth
politics.’’'” Judged by intellectual standards, however, the Science Wars
are misguided. In this book, I will present a way out o
---
 the Wars by
developing a conception o
---
 social science based on a contemporary
interpretation o
---
 the Aristotelian concept o
---
 phronesis, variously translated
as prudence or practical wisdom. In Aristotle’s words phronesis is a “true
state, reasoned, and capable o
---
 action with regard to things that are good
or bad 
---
or man.”’!’ Phronesis goes beyond both analytical, scienti
---
ic knowledge (episteme) and technical knowledge or know-how (techne) and involves judgments and decisions made in the manner o
---
 a virtuoso social
and political actor. I will argue that phronesis is commonly involved in
social practice, and that there
---
ore attempts to reduce social science and
theory either to episteme or techne, or to comprehend them in those terms,
are misguided.

By introducing phronesis into the discussion o
---
 what social science is

The Science Wars: a way out

3

and can be, we will see that Lewontin and others are right, albeit perhaps
not 
---
or the reasons they believe, when they say that social science has set
itsel
---
 an impossible task when it attempts to emulate natural science and
produce explanatory and predictive, that is, epistemic, theory. We will
also see, however, that this conclusion does not imply the o
---
t-seen image
o
---
 impotent social sciences versus potent natural sciences, which is at the
core o
---
 the Science Wars. This image derives 
---
rom the 
---
act that both types
o
---
 science tend to be compared in terms o
---
 their epistemic qualities. This
book will argue that such a comparison is misleading. The two types o
---

science have their respective strengths and weaknesses along 
---
undamentally di
---

---
erent dimensions, a point which Aristotle demonstrated but
which has since been 
---
orgotten. At present, social science is locked in a

---
ight it cannot hope to win, because it has accepted terms that are
sel
---
-de
---
eating. We will see that in their role as phronesis, the social sciences
are strongest where the natural sciences are weakest: just as the social
sciences have not contributed much to explanatory and predictive theory,
neither have the natural sciences contributed to the re
---
lexive analysis and
discussion o
---
 values and interests, which is the prerequisite 
---
or an enlightened political, economic, and cultural development in any society,
and which is at the core o
---
 phronesis. This should also be the core o
---
 social
science i
---
 we want to transcend the current malaise o
---
 the Science Wars.
Virtue lost

Aristotle, the philosopher o
---
 phronesis par excellence, never elaborated his
conception o
---
 phronesis to include explicit considerations o
---
 power. HansGeorg Gadamer’s authoritative and contemporary conception o
---
 phronesis also overlooks issues o
---
 power.'* Yet as Richard Bernstein points out,
i
---
 we are to think about what can be done to the problems and risks o
---
 our
time, we must advance 
---
rom the original conception o
---
 phronesis to one
explicitly including power.'? Un
---
ortunately, Bernstein himsel
---
 has not
integrated his work on phronesis with issues o
---
 power. Nor, to my knowledge, has anyone else. I will argue that in modern society, con
---
lict and
power are phenomena constitutive o
---
 social and political inquiry. And I
will develop the classic concept o
---
 phronesis to include issues o
---
 power.
Aristotle, in arguing that natural and social science are and should be
di
---

---
erent ventures, discusses the three intellectual virtues, episteme, techne,
and phronesis. Whereas episteme is 
---
ound in the modern words “epistemology’ and “‘epistemic,”’ and techne in “‘technology”’ and “‘technical,”’ it is
indicative o
---
 the degree to which thinking in the social sciences has
we
allowed itsel
---
 to be colonized by natural and technical science that
phronesis,
today do not even have a word 
---
or the one intellectual virtue,

4

Making social science matter

social and political
which Aristotle saw not only as the necessary basis 
---
or
virtues. Phronesis is
inquiry, but as the most important o
---
 the intellectual
umental rationalmost important because it is that activity by which instr
cing is crucial
ity is balanced by value-rationality, and because such balan
according to
to the sustained happiness o
---
 the citizens in any society,
en the
Aristotle. In what 
---
ollows we will redress the imbalance betwe
nt
intellectual virtues by submitting the concept o
---
 phronesis to a curre
reinterpretation in terms o
---
 the needs o
---
 contemporary social science. The
goal is to help restore social science to its classical position as a practical,
intellectual activity aimed at clari
---
ying the problems, risks, and possibilities we 
---
ace as humans and societies, and at contributing to social and
political praxis.
A brie
---
 overview

Based on a critique o
---
 cognitivism and naturalism, Part one o
---
 the book
shows why social science never has been, and probably never will be, able
to develop the type o
---
 explanatory and predictive theory that is the ideal
and hallmark o
---
 natural science. Chapter two demonstrates that context
and judgment are irreducibly central to understanding human action. On
this basis, 
---
ollowing works by Hubert Drey
---
us, Pierre Bourdieu, and
Harold Gar
---
inkel, chapters three and 
---
our explore the question o
---

whether a theory o
---
 context and judgment is possible. The answer to this
question is negative and the conclusion is that social science emulation o
---

natural science is a cul-de-sac; mainstream social theory and social
science methodology stand in need o
---
 reorientation.
Part two is an attempt at such a reorientation based on phronesis.
Chapter 
---
ive introduces Aristotle’s original thoughts on the subject and

explores the relationship between phronesis and social science. The 
---
ollowing chapters then develop the concept o
---
 phronesis on three 
---
ronts to
make 
---
or a more contemporary interpretation. First, chapter six takes its
point o
---
 departure in Aristotle’s insight that case knowledge is crucial to
the practice o
---
 phronesis; on this basis the chapter clari
---
ies the status and
uses o
---
 case studies in social science. Second, based on works by Michel

Foucault, Jiirgen Habermas, and Friedrich Nietzsche, chapters seven and
eight elaborate the classical conception o
---
 phronesis to include considerations On power, thus expanding the classical concept 
---
rom one o
---
 values
to one o
---
 values and power. Third, chapter nine 
---
urther re
---
ines the

approach by developing a set o
---
 methodological guidelines 
---
or doing what
I call “‘phronetic social science.” Chapter ten contains illustrations and
examples o
---
 such an approach, while Chapter eleven sums up the perspective o
---
 the book.

The Science Wars: a way out

5

My aims with this book are simply to call attention to a central problem in
the social sciences and to outline a possible answer. I see the problem —
the 
---
act that the social sciences have not had the type o
---
 theoretical and
methodological success that the natural sciences have — as 
---
airly well
de
---
ined and well documented. The answer, however, seems less clear,
and I do not think there is a single answer. My own attempt at an answer —

phronetic social science — should be considered only one attempt among
many possible. It should also be seen as only a 
---
irst step that will undoubtedly need 
---
urther theoretical and methodological re
---
inement, just
as it will need to be developed through 
---
urther practical employment in
social-science research.'® Despite such quali
---
ications, I hope the reader
will agree that given what is at stake — social sciences that can hold their
own in the Science Wars, in the academic community, and in society at
large — the attempt at re
---
orming these sciences is worth making.

Rationality, body, and intuition in human

learning

Our task is to broaden our reasoning to make it capable o
---
 grasping
what, in ourselves and others, precedes and exceeds reason.
Maurice Merleau-Ponty

Context is central to understanding what social science is and can be.
This chapter asks, ““What role does context play in human knowledge and
skills?” Philosophy o
---
 science and epistemology typically pose questions
such as: ““What is knowledge?’’; ““What can we know?’’; ‘Under what
conditions can we know that we know?” Here we will approach the
question o
---
 knowledge by asking the more dynamic question: ‘““How do
people acquire knowledge and skills?”’ It is by addressing this question
that we begin to understand the problem o
---
 context.
The intention here is not to outline and analyze all possible ways in
which people acquire knowledge and skills, nor shall we review the many
schools and theories that exist in this area. Rather we will deal with a
single phenomenology o
---
 human learning as 
---
ormulated by Hubert and
Stuart Drey
---
us.’ This particular phenomenology has been chosen because it is especially use
---
ul 
---
or understanding the linkage between knowl-

edge and context, and because it directly addresses the question o
---

whether knowledge about human activity can be context-independent.
The answer to this latter question is decisive 
---
or an understanding and
response to two 
---
undamental epistemological questions in the study o
---

human activity: “Are theory and epistemology possible in social science?”’
“Can social and political science be scienti
---
ic in the same sense as is
natural science?”
The 
---
irst part o
---
 the chapter reviews the phenomenology o
---
 human
learning, the so-called Drey
---
us model. We will then discuss the model’s
implications 
---
or social science.

10

Why social science has 
---
ailed as science

Competence and virtuosity in human learning
on a
Some years ago in the United States, an experiment was conducted
g
group o
---
 paramedics. Video 
---
ilms were made o
---
 six persons administerin
.
cardiopulmonary resuscitation (CPR) to victims o
---
 acute heart 
---
ailure
Five o
---
 the six were inexperienced trainees just learning CPR, while the

sixth was a paramedic with long experience in emergency li
---
e-saving
techniques. The 
---
ilms were shown to three groups o
---
 subjects: paramedics

with practical experience, students being trained in this 
---
ield, and instruc-

tors in li
---
e-saving techniques. Each subject was asked the 
---
ollowing question: “‘Who o
---
 the six persons shown in the 
---
ilms would you choose to
resuscitate you i
---
 you were the victim o
---
 such an accident?” Among the
group o
---
 experienced paramedics, 90 percent chose the one experienced
paramedic 
---
rom the 
---
ilms. The students chose “correctly” in only 50
percent o
---
 the cases. Finally, and perhaps surprisingly, the instructors in
resuscitation had poorer results than either the experienced paramedics
or the students, choosing the experienced paramedic in only 30 percent o
---


the cases.”
What 
---
orm o
---
 rationality led the instructors to achieve such a poor
per
---
ormance? And what mechanisms lay behind the experienced paramedics’ well-developed ability to choose correctly? These questions will
be dealt with in the 
---
ollowing discussion.

Context, experience, and intuition
In the introduction to this chapter I described a study in which a group o
---

---

had been victim o
---

---

---
ilms shown. Practical experience consists precisely in an individual’s
ability to readily recognize skill and virtuoso expertise. Teachers in rescuing li
---

---
y the expert paramedic, they were
even worse than the group o
---

A
---

---

---

---

students in CPR. The teachers’ concept o

---
ilms as “‘good”’ because, as novices, they closely
and consciously

students could do little else. In 70 percent o
---

not identi
---
y the experienced rescuer because this individual, being truly
experienced — an expert — had gone beyond rule-based behavior.
The example would be trivial i

in the health sector or only in the United States. Regrettably, the pervasiveness o

The theoretical ambition

The natural-science model has been, and continues to be, an ideal shared
by several traditions in the study o
---

---

has now been argued that the ideal does not even work 
---

to so many scholars. There is a logical simplicity to the natural science
paradigm, and the natural sciences’ impressive material results speak 
---

by which we have attempted to achieve mastery over nature, technology,
and over our own conditions o

---

consequence o

The natural sciences are relatively cumulative. Thomas Kuhn’s

---

---

Periods with scienti
---

---

stable, normal-science research, in that it is via the daily normal “‘puzzle
solving” that the anomalies and contradictions appear which gradually
undermine the original paradigm. The resulting crisis continues until the
anomalies and contradictions can be explained within a new paradigm;
that is, until researchers experience “‘‘the pieces suddenly sorting themselves out and coming together in a new way,” to use Kuhn’s words.”
The result is a new paradigm around which there is again general agreement and on the basis o
---

conducted.

Meaning-giving and meaninglessness
In The Order o
---

---

society, what he calls the “human sciences,” understands human beings

---
or theoretical and empirical

---

etation in

the studying to humankind’s double role as both meaning giver (subje

meaning is rendered.’ I
---

necessary to understand both poles o
---

standing possible drives the study o

instability.” Hence, the human sciences

---

---

---

They are always animated, there

so much to generalize themselves or make themselves more precise as to be

constantly demysti
---

Brie
---
ly summarized, Foucault’s argument contains three elements. First,

he points out that the study o
---

characterized by a meaning giver and object duality, and that this duality
has been an obstacle to the development o
---

sciences. For Foucault, no science can objectivize the phenomenon
which makes it possible. Stability cannot be achieved when that phenomenon which is the locus o
---

object o
---
 science. Second, Foucault argues — with the help o
---

---

---

there
---
ore not necessarily irrevocably connected with every 
---
orm o
---

science.
Finally, at the time when The Order o
---

Foucault believed that the problems o
---

Like many French intellectuals, Foucault at this time saw structuralism —
as 
---

and others — as a promising research program 
---

---

---

objective, stable, and cumulative human sciences. As is well known,
however, the hopes o

reason 
---

himsel

---

despite its high hopes and ambitious promises, was not up to the task o

creating objective human sciences any better than was behaviorism,
cognitivism, or other schools in the study o
---

Beyond theory
It is worth reiterating that Drey
---

---

---

---

social science cannot become scienti
---
ic in the sense o
---
 normal science. On
the other hand, the 
---
act that this cannot be proven does not make the
social sciences’ instability a historical coincidence, nor must we conclude

Context counts

47

that their lack o
---
 maturity is due to the complexity o
---
 their object o
---
 study.
The 
---
our arguments about the possibility and impossibility o
---
 social
science theory outlined in this and the previous chapter can be summarized as 
---
ollows:
1. The pre-paradigmatic argument, 
---
ollowing the early Rorty, is correct
inso
---
ar as there is, in principle, nothing to prevent social science 
---
rom
becoming normal science in the 
---
uture. That is, it has not been demonstrated that it is zmpossible 
---
or social science to become normal science.
Yet 
---
rom here it is di
---

---
icult, and perhaps misguided, to believe that this
will actually come to pass. At present there exist no normal-science
theories in the social sciences, and there is no reason to believe a priori
in the existence o
---
 the abstract context-independent concepts which
such theories would presuppose. Hence, the abstract normal-science
possibility — which is but an unrealized possibility — is not especially use
---
ul
in understanding the problems o
---
 the social sciences as science.
2. The hermeneutic—phenomenological argument o
---
 Anthony Giddens and
Harold Gar
---
inkel demonstrates that the study o
---
 human activity must be
based on people’s situational sel
---
-interpretation, and that such studies
can only be as stable as these interpretations.
3. The historical contingency argument o
---
 Foucault in The Order o
---
 Things
is that stable and cumulative sciences which study human behavior are
not possible because humans both constitute these sciences and are at the
same time their object. No science can objectivize the skills which make it
possible. On this point Foucault is correct, but he errs in saying that the
social sciences are plagued by these problems only during a given historical period. The problems are more general than Foucault depicts them.
4. The “‘tacit skills’? argument o
---
 Drey
---
us and Bourdieu is the most
general and most rigorous o
---
 the 
---
our. It says that stable and cumulative
social sciences presume a necessary but apparently impossible theory o
---

human background skills. The theory is necessary in order to ensure
predictability. It seems impossible because human skills are contextdependent and cannot be reduced to rules, whereas a theory must be 
---
ree
o
---
 context and have rules. The context cannot be excluded because, as

Bourdieu shows, context de
---
ines the type o
---
 phenomenon which the
theory encompasses. However, a theory must necessarily be context-
---
ree,
otherwise it is not a general theory. This is the dilemma in which the social
sciences 
---
ind themselves, a dilemma which has existed since their incep-

tion, and which, it appears, will continue.'’ So 
---
ar cognitivists and others
have 
---
ailed in their attempts to analyze context as just very complex sets o
---

rules. Context-dependence appears to entail an open-ended, dependent

48

Why social science has 
---
ailed as science

relation between contexts and actions and interpretations that cannot be
brought under rule-based closure. For humans, ‘ bene has a logic
which is not that o
---
 logic,” in the words o
---
 Bourdieu.'®
In the conventional view o
---
 ideal science, it is precisely normal science
that lies at the core o
---
 scienti
---
ic identity. It is there
---
ore not surprising that
researchers tend to aspire to the normal-scienti
---
ic ideal. [hat the ideal
does not work in practice 
---
or social science and that there is nothing,
which indicates that it ever will is another story. The goal is clear, even
though it cannot be achieved. I
---
 the normal-scienti
---
ic ideal were abandoned, we would be without both goals and results, where at present only
the results are lacking. The two examples o
---
 the Science Wars mentioned
in chapter one, that is, “‘Sokal’s hoax’? and the US National Opinion
Research Center study o
---
 sexual practices illustrate the vigor with which
the question o
---
 whether social science can achieve Kuhnian normality is
discussed, and how strongly many social scientists hold on to the normalscience ideal, despite evidence that the ideal has not been and probably
cannot be achieved.
Without 
---
aith in the possibility o
---
 theory and epistemology in social
science, many scholars 
---
ear that the door would become open 
---
or scienti
---
ic relativism and nihilism. In reality, the door is already wide open 
---
or
relativistic and nihilistic tendencies, inso
---
ar as epistemically oriented
social scientists ignore the 
---
act that epistemic theory cannot serve as a
bulwark against relativism and nihilism since such theory, as we have
shown, does currently not exist and can probably never come to exist.
(For more on relativism, see chapter nine.)
With these considerations, we have repealed the conventional normalscience ideal 
---
or social science. The question o
---
 what can be put in its
place remains to be answered. Cognitivism, 
---
unctionalism, structuralism,
and neopositivism have so 
---
ar 
---
ailed to produce epistemic theory, as have
other objectivist strands o
---
 social science. Hermeneutics by itsel
---
 is inadequate. Critical Theory has long been preoccupied with Habermas’s
attempt to 
---
ormulate a normative discourse theory and its consequences

---
or politics and jurisprudence. What should researchers and students do
in this situation? What are the consequences 
---
or our practical work? For
the choice o
---
 problems we study? The choice o
---
 methodology? What are
the consequences 
---
or social science aside 
---
rom the obvious 
---
act that
context must have a more central position, and theory a less central one?

It is in answering these questions that we can learn 
---
rom Aristotle’s
considerations in the Ethics regarding what he calls the “intellectual
virtues”’ o
---
 episteme, techne, and phronesis.
Be
---
ore describing Aristotle’s thought, I want to conclude this chapter

Context counts

49

by pointing out some o
---
 the pit
---
alls one encounters when attempting to
address the above questions. The purpose o
---
 this and the previous chapter has been to deconstruct the conventional scienti
---
ic ideal 
---
or the social
sciences, with its emphasis on theory and context-independence. In a
critique o
---
 deconstruction, the Danish linguist, Per Aage Brandt, has
pointed out that making a philosophical principle out o
---
 di
---

---
erence is to
invalidate our knowledge, and that understanding between humans represents the “‘victory o
---
 signs over di
---

---
erence.’’!® In similar 
---
ashion, one
might say that 
---
ocusing on the particular, the non-rule-based, and on
context is to dismiss scienti
---
ic knowledge, and that scienti
---
ic knowledge is
precisely the victory o
---
 the general — o
---
 rules — over the particular.
In my judgment, this point o
---
 view, while containing an important
insight, is still unproductive, 
---
or it sets against each other two equally
invalid extremes: di
---

---
erence versus sign, the particular versus the rulebased. Dualisms like these may 
---
acilitate thinking and writing, but they
inhibit understanding by implying a certain neatness that is rarely 
---
ound
in lived li
---
e. I
---
 not meaningless, it is counterproductive to meaning to
speak o
---
 “the victory o
---
 signs over di
---

---
erence” or o
---
 rules over the
particular. There are signs, and there is di
---

---
erence. There are rules, and
there is the particular. This much can be observed phenomenologically,
and it is this phenomenology which is our 
---
ocus. To amputate one side in
these pairs o
---
 phenomena into a dualistic “‘either—or’’ is to amputate our
understanding. Rather than the “‘either—or,”’ we should develop a nondualistic and pluralistic ‘“‘both—and.’’ Hence, we should not criticize
rules, logic, signs, and rationality in themselves. We should criticize only
the dominance o
---
 these phenomena to the exclusion o
---
 others in modern
society and in social science. Conversely, it would be equally problematic
i
---
 rules, logic, signs, and rationality were marginalized by the concrete, by

di
---

---
erence, and by the particular. This latter problem, however, is presently 
---
ar less pressing than the 
---
ormer.

Values in social and political inquiry

---
 you want a description o
---
 our age, here is one: the civilization o

Richard Livingstone

Value-rationality and instrumental rationality
Just as social science has not been able to contribute with Kuhnian
normal science and predictive theory to scienti
---
ic development, so natural
science has had little to o
---

---
er to the re
---
lexive analysis o
---
 goals, values, and
interests that is a precondition 
---
or an enlightened development in any
society. However, where natural science is weak, social science is strong,
and vice versa. For Aristotle, the most important task o
---
 social and
political studies was to develop society’s value-rationality vis-a-vis its
scienti
---
ic and technical rationality. Aristotle did not doubt that the 
---
irst
type o
---
 rationality was the most important and ought to in
---
luence the
second. Since Aristotle’s time, however, this view has receded into the
background, especially a
---
ter the Enlightenment and modernity installed
instrumental rationality in a dominant position in both science and
society. Social thinkers as diverse as Max Weber, Michel Foucault, and
Jirgen Habermas have pointed out that 
---
or more than two centuries
value-rationality (Wertrationalitat) has increasingly given way to instrumental rationality (Zweckrationalitat).' In the words o
---
 Richard Livingstone: “i
---
 you want a description o
---
 our age, here is one: the civilization o
---

means without ends.’ Today the Aristotelian question o
---
 balancing
instrumental rationality with value-rationality is 
---
orcing its way back to
the 
---
oreground. Problems with both biosphere and sociosphere indicate
that social and political development based on instrumental rationality
alone is not sustainable.
I have argued elsewhere that the Rationalist Turn — the narrowing o
---

modern society’s notion o
---
 rationality to a predominantly instrumental
one — is constitutive o
---
 what has been called the “‘risk society.”’’ Even
though we need not go 
---
urther back than the heyday o
---
 logical positivism
in the 1950s and 1960s in order to see the rationalist ideal as dominant in
53

54

How social science can matter again

science and society, and even though this ideal also occupies a prominent
position today in key scienti
---
ic growth areas such as the new in
---
ormation
sciences, cognitive science, linguistics, and computer science, many
people now believe that alternatives to instrumental rationalism are
needed. The precise content o
---
 such alternatives, however, remains
vague. The Rationalist Turn has been so radical that possible alternatives,
which might have existed previously, are beyond our curregt vision, just
as centuries o
---
 rationalist socialization seems to have undermined the
ability o
---
 individuals and society to even conceptualize a nonrationalist
present and 
---
uture.
One way o
---
 dealing with this situation is to study the era prior to the
Rationalist Turn. We will not simply inquire about what it is we lost to
rationalism. Nor will we ask how a less rationalistic past could be resurrected. Rather, we will probe the question o
---
 what we might learn about
the present 
---
rom the past, and how, in this light, our modern concept o
---

rationality might be re
---
ormulated and extended. This strategy inevitably
leads back to the Greek philosophers. Socrates, Plato, and Aristotle are
o
---
ten seen as 
---
ore
---
athers o
---
 modern science and o
---
 present day rationalism. Aristotle, however, distinguished explicitly between several “intellectual virtues,”’ o
---
 which epistemic science, with its emphasis on theories,
analysis, and universals, was but one, and not even the most important.
Aristotle added intellectual virtues dealing with context, practice, experience, common sense, intuition, and practical wisdom, especially the
intellectual virtue named phronesis.
It is not my purpose to subject Aristotle or other Greek philosophers to
a historical analysis in the traditional sense. Nor do I seek to analyze the
society in which they worked, how their work was used, etc. I do not view
Greek philosophy or classical Greek society as an ideal to be emulated
today. Such an exercise would be sterile utopian idealism, and basically
useless. I agree with Michel Foucault that “this idea that the Greek

civilization is something like a model, which has been 
---
orgotten .. .
doesn’t make a lot o
---
 sense, since history is history. There is no way, no
hope, no positive meaning in turning back on something.’” Still,
Foucault goes on to observe that in antiquity we have a unique example o
---

an ethics that, unlike ours, was not related either to religion, or to civil
law, or to science. ‘‘And I think,”’ says Foucault, ‘‘that we are now at the
point where we recognize that those three re
---
erences 
---
or our ethics are not
su
---

---
icient.’ In the Greeks we have an example o
---
 a culture in which ethics
have been very strong; the “‘philosophy o
---
 ethics”? is one o
---
 the major
achievements o
---
 this culture, and something we can learn 
---
rom today.
But, says Foucault, our solutions have to be very di
---

---
erent 
---
rom those o
---

the Greeks.°

Values in social and political inquiry

55

Foucault here talks about ethics in relation to “‘an aesthetics o
---
 existence,”’ that is, the relationship you have to yoursel
---
 when you act. Aristotle, in discussing phronesis, is mainly talking about ethics in relation to
social and political praxis, that is, the relationship you have to society when
you act.’ In chapter eight we will return to the relationship between
Foucault and Aristotle. For now we will ask what we can learn about our
current situation 
---
rom a contemporary reading o
---
 Aristotle and his considerations in the Ethics about the intellectual virtues o
---
 episteme, techne,
and phronesis. A
---
ter having deconstructed the modern concepts o
---
 rationality and social science in chapters two to 
---
our, the purpose is now to

start reconstructing these concepts.
Aristotle is the classic philosopher o
---
 phronesis but he never elaborated
his conception o
---
 phronesis to include explicit considerations on power.
Similarly, Hans-Georg Gadamer’s conception o
---
 phronesis also overlooks
issues o
---
 power. Yet as Richard Bernstein points out, “no practical
discussion is going to take place unless you understand the relevance o
---

phronesis. But no practical philosophy can be adequate 
---
or our time unless
it con
---
ronts the analysis o
---
 power.’’® This, then, is a methodological aim

o
---
 what 
---
ollows: to develop the classic concept o
---
 phronesis to include
issues o
---
 power. In this chapter the classic concept o
---
 phronesis is explicated. In chapters seven and eight this concept is expanded to include
issues o
---
 power.
Aristotle on episteme, techne, and phronesis
The term ‘“‘epistemic science”’ derives 
---
rom the intellectual virtue that
Aristotle calls episteme, and which is generally translated as “‘science”’ or
“scienti
---
ic knowledge.’” Aristotle de
---
ines episteme in this manner:
What science [episteme] is . . . will be clear 
---
rom the 
---
ollowing argument. We all
assume that what we know cannot be otherwise than it is, whereas in the case o
---

things that may be otherwise, when they have passed out o
---
 our view we can no
longer tell whether they exist or not. There
---
ore, the object o
---
 scienti
---
ic knowledge
is o
---
 necessity. There
---
ore it is eternal . . . Induction introduces us to 
---
irst
principles and universals, while deduction starts 
---
rom universals . . . Thus
scienti
---
ic knowledge is a demonstrative state, (i.e., a state o
---
 mind capable o
---

demonstrating what it knows) . . . i.e., a person has scienti
---
ic knowledge when his
belie
---
 is conditioned in a certain way, and the 
---
irst principles are known to him;
because i
---
 they are not better known to him than the conclusion drawn 
---
rom
them, he will have knowledge only incidentally. - This may serve as a description

o
---
 scienti
---
ic knowledge.'”

Episteme thus concerns universals and the production o
---
 knowledge which

is invariable in time and space, and which is achieved with the aid o
---


56

How social science can matter again

analytical rationality. Episteme corresponds to the modern scienti
---
ic ideal
as expressed in natural science. In Socrates and Plato, and subsequently
in the Enlightenment tradition, this scienti
---
ic ideal became dominant.

The ideal has come close to being the only legitimate view o
---
 what
constitutes genuine science, such that even intellectual activities like
social science, which are not and probably never can be scienti
---
ic in this
sense, have 
---
ound

themselves

compelled

to strive 
---
or and legitimate

themselves in terms o
---
 this Enlightenment ideal.
Whereas episteme resembles our ideal modern scienti
---
ic project, techne
and phronesis denote two contrasting roles o
---
 intellectual work. Techne can
be translated into English as “‘art”’ in the sense o
---
 ‘‘cra
---
t’’; a cra
---
tsman is
also an artisan. For Aristotle, both techne and phronesis are connected with
the concept o
---
 truth, as is episteme. Aristotle says the 
---
ollowing regarding
techne:
[S]ince (e.g.) building is an art [techne] and is essentially a reasoned productive
state, and since there is no art that is not a state o
---
 this kind, and no state o
---
 this
kind that is not an art, it 
---
ollows that art is the same as a productive state that is

truly reasoned. Every art is concerned with bringing something into being, and
the practice o
---
 an art is the study o
---
 how to bring into being something that is
capable either o
---
 being or o
---

to be o
---
 necessity that art is concerned [this is the domain o
---

natural objects (because these have their origin in themselves) ... Art...

operate[s] in the sphere o
---
 the variable."’
Techne is thus cra
---
t and art, and as an activity it is concrete, variable, and

context-dependent. The objective o
---
 techne is application o
---
 technical
knowledge and skills according to a pragmatic instrumental rationality,
what Foucault calls “‘a practical rationality governed by a conscious

goal.”’!
Whereas episteme concerns theoretical know why and techne denotes
technical know how, phronesis emphasizes practical knowledge and practical ethics. Phronesis is o
---
ten translated as “‘prudence”’ or “‘practical
common sense.”’ Let us again examine what Aristotle has to say:
We may grasp the nature o
---
 prudence [phronesis] i
---
 we consider what sort o
---
 people
we call prudent. Well, it is thought to be the mark o
---
 a prudent man to be able to
deliberate rightly about what is good and advantageous . . . But nobody deliberates about things that are invariable .. . So . . . prudence cannot be science or art;
not science [episteme] because what can be done is a variable (it may be done in
di
---

---
erent ways, or not done at all), and not art [techne] because action and
production are generically di
---

---
erent. For production aims at an end other than
itsel
---
; but this is impossible in the case o
---

well. What remains, then, is that it is a true state, reasoned, and capable o
---
 action

with regard to things that are good or bad 
---
or man. .. We consider that this quality

Values in social and political inquiry

57

belongs to those who understand the management o
---
 households or states.
[Italics in the original]

The person possessing practical wisdom (phronimos) has knowledge o
---

how to behave in each particular circumstance that can never be equated
with or reduced to knowledge o
---
 general truths. Phronesis is a sense o
---
 the
ethically practical rather than a kind o
---
 science. Where rational humans

---
or Plato are moved by the cosmic order, 
---
or Aristotle they are moved bya
sense o
---
 the proper order among the ends we pursue. This sense cannot
be articulated in terms o
---

phronesis.'* Here, in Aristotle’s original description o

get the impression that phronesis and the choices it involves in concrete
circumstances are always good. Later, a
---
ter we have introduced the
concept o
---

---

not necessarily the case. Choices must be deemed good (or bad) in
relation to certain values and interests in order 
---
or good and bad to have
meaning.
In sum, the three intellectual virtues episteme, techne, and phronesis can
be characterized as 
---
ollows:
Episteme Scienti
---
ic knowledge. Universal, invariable, contextindependent. Based on general analytical rationality. The original concept is known today 
---
rom the terms “epistemology”’
and “‘epistemic.”’
Techne
Cra
---
t/art. Pragmatic, variable, context-dependent.
Oriented toward production. Based on practical instrumental
rationality governed by a conscious goal. The original concept
appears today in terms such as “‘technique,”’ “‘technical,”’ and
“technology.”
Phronesis Ethics. Deliberation about values with re
---
erence to
praxis. Pragmatic, variable, context-dependent. Oriented toward action. Based on practical value-rationality. The original

concept has no analogous contemporary term.
The priority o
---
 the particular
Phronesis thus concerns the analysis o
---
 values — “‘things that are good or
bad 
---
or man” — as a point o
---
 departure 
---
or action. Phronesis is that
intellectual activity most relevant to praxis. It 
---
ocuses on what is variable,
on that which cannot be encapsulated by universal rules, on speci
---
ic
cases. Phronesis requires an interaction between the general and the
concrete; it requires consideration, judgment, and choice.'? More than
anything else, phronesis requires experience. About the importance o
---

speci
---
ic experience Aristotle says:

also take
(P]rudence [phronesis] is not concerned with universals only; it must

its sphere in particular circumstances. That is why
possess theoretical knowledge are more e

Some interpretations o

judgment” as one o

With his thoughts on the intellectual virtues, Aristotle emphasizes
properties o
---

---

particular and the situationally dependent are emphasized over the universal and over rules. The concrete and the practical are emphasized over
the theoretical. It is what Martha Nussbaum calls the “priority o

particular”’ in Aristotle’s thinking.”° Aristotle practices what he preaches

---
undamental concerning human 
---
unctioning. Both aspects
are typical o
---
 many Classical philosophers.

Values in social and political inquiry

Later on we will return to these points. Here we may simply conclude

Where natural science is weak and social science strong

Social science can also contribute to social development as techne in

act as controlling, repressive, and legitimating. Which role they come to

theory building.
The problems o

---

Misunderstanding 1. General, theoretical (context-independent)
knowledge is more valuable than concrete, practical (contextdependent) knowledge.

Misunderstanding 2. One cannot generalize on the basis o
---

individual case; there

---

Misunderstanding 3. The case study is most use

Socrates, Plato, and cases

Validity and truth are ensured where the participants in a given

Aristotle, Nietzsche, and Foucault

““[P]hilosophy begins with Aristotle,’’ Foucault says provocatively, not

Writing wirkliche Historie, the researcher operates on the basis o

---

(1) Researchers are both involved in, and partially produced by, the same

---

(3) The meaning o

---

Here it is worth noting that Foucault does not accept Jacques Derrida’s

the text inde

---

---

disciplines interpretation.

For Foucault, the association between political philosophy and social
theory on the one hand and political practice on the other thus tends to be
a weak one. From this perspective, Foucault states that attempts to solve
the problems o

against rationalization and repression, which both Foucault, Weber, and

Foucault’s position does not mean that ‘‘anything goes”’ in a theoretical context. Nor does it imply that theory is not important.’’ Rather, it
means that theories, and conceptualization in general, must be constantly
con

Focusing on values

By de
---
inition, phronetic researchers 
---
ocus on values; 
---
or example, by
taking their point o
---
 departure in the classic value-rational questions:
Where are we going? Is it desirable? What should be done? As described
in chapter 
---
ive, in the discussion o
---

objective is to balance instrumental rationality with value-rationality by
increasing the capacity o
---
 individuals, organizations, and society to think
and act in value-rational terms. Focusing on values, the phronetic researcher is 
---
orced to 
---
ace the question o
---
 
---
oundationalism versus relativism, that is, the view that central values exist that can be rationally and

universally grounded, versus the view that one set o
---
 values is just as good
as another.
Phronetic researchers reject both o
---
 these “‘isms”’ and replace them by
contextualism, that is, by situational ethics. Distancing themselves 
---
rom

---
oundationalism does not leave phronetic researchers normless, however.
They take their point o
---
 departure in their attitude to the situation in the
society being studied. They seek to ensure that such an attitude is not
based on idiosyncratic morality or personal pre
---
erences, but instead on a
common view among a speci
---
ic re
---
erence group to which the researchers
re
---
er. For phronetic researchers, the socially and historically conditioned
context — and not the rational and universal grounding which is desired by
certain philosophers, but which is not yet achieved — constitutes the most
e
---

---
ective bulwark against relativism and nihilism. Phronetic researchers
realize that our sociality and history is the only 
---
oundation we have, the
only solid ground under our 
---
eet. And that this socio-historical 
---
oundation is 
---
ully adequate 
---
or our work as social scientists.
As regards validity, phronetic research is based on interpretation and is
open 
---
or testing in relation to other interpretations and other research.
But one interpretation is not just as good as another, which would be the
case 
---
or relativism. Every interpretation must be built upon claims o
---

validity, and the procedures ensuring validity are as demanding 
---
or
phronetic research as 
---
or any other activity in the social and political
sciences. Phronetic researchers also oppose the view that any one among

Methodological guidelines 
---
or a re
---
ormed social science

131

a number o
---

interpretation. As emphasized by Alexander Nehamas, the key point is
the
establishment o
---
 a better alternative, where “‘better” is de
---
ined accord
ing
to sets o
---
 validity claims.’ I
---
 a better interpretation demonstrates the
previous interpretation to be “‘merely”’ interpretation, this new interpretation remains valid until another, still better interpretation is produced
which can reduce the previous interpretation to “‘merely”’ interpretation.
This is the procedure which a community o
---

---
ollow
in working together to put certain interpretations o
---
 social and political
li
---
e ahead o
---

below). The procedure describes not an interpretive or relativistic

Placing power at the core o
---
 analysis
Besides 
---
ocusing on the three value-rational questions mentioned above,
which are the classical Aristotelian questions, a contemporary reading o
---

phronesis also poses questions about power and outcomes: Who gains,
and who loses? Through what kinds o
---
 power relations? What possibilities
are available to change existing power relations? And is it desirable to do
so? O
---
 what kinds o
---
 power relations are those asking these questions
themselves a part? Phronetic research poses these questions with the
intention o
---
 avoiding the voluntarism and idealism typical o
---
 so much
ethical thinking. The main question is not only the Weberian: ‘““Who
governs?”’ posed by Robert Dahl and most other students o
---
 power. It is
also the Nietzschean question: What “‘governmental rationalities”’ are at
work when those who govern govern? With these questions and with
the 
---
ocus on value-rationality, phronetic researchers relate explicitly
to a primary context o
---
 values and power. Combining the best o
---

a Nietzschean—Foucauldian interpretation o
---
 power with the best o
---
 a
Weberian—Dahlian one, the analysis o
---
 power would be guided by a
conception o
---
 power that can be characterized by six 
---
eatures:

(1) Power is seen as productive and positive and not only as restrictive
and negative.
(2) Power is viewed as a dense net o
---
 omnipresent relations and not only
as localized in “‘centers’’ and institutions, or as an entity one can
*“possess.””’

(3) The concept o
---
 power is seen as ultradynamic; power is not only

132

How social science can matter again

something one appropriates, but also something one reappropriates
and exercises in a constant back-and-
---
orth movement in relations o
---

strength, tactics, and strategies.
(4) Knowledge and power, truth and power, rationality and power are
analytically inseparable 
---
rom each other; power produces knowledge,
and knowledge produces power.
(5) The central question is how power is exercised, and H808only who has
power, and why they have it; the 
---
ocus is on process in addition to

structure.
(6) Power is studied with a point o
---
 departure in small questions, “
---
lat
and empirical,’ not only, nor primarily, with a point o
---
 departure in
“big questions.’
Analyses o
---
 power 
---
ollowing this 
---
ormat cannot be equated with a general
analytics o
---
 every possible power relation. Other approaches and other
interpretations are possible. They can, however, serve as a possible and
productive point o
---
 departure 
---
or dealing with questions o
---
 power in
doing phronesis.

Getting close to reality
Donald Campbell, Charles Lindblom, and others have noted that the
development o
---
 social research is inhibited by the 
---
act that researchers
tend to work with problems in which the answer to the question “I
---
 you
are wrong about this, who will notice?” is ‘“Nobody.’’* Mary Timney
Bailey calls the outcome o
---
 this type o
---
 research
“‘‘so what’ results.’”
Phronetic researchers seek to transcend this problem o
---

anchoring their research in the context studied and thereby ensuring a
hermeneutic “
---
usion o
---
 horizons.’’ This applies both to contemporary
and historical studies. For contemporary studies one gets close to the
phenomenon or group whom one studies during data collection, and
remains close during the phases o
---
 data analysis, 
---
eedback, and publication o
---
 results. Combined with the above-mentioned 
---
ocus on relations o
---

values and power, this strategy typically creates interest by outside pareee

ties, and even outside stakeholders, in the research. These parties will test
and evaluate the research in various ways. The researchers will conscious-

ly expose themselves to reactions 
---
rom their surroundings — both positive
and negative — and may derive bene
---
it 
---
rom the learning e
---

---
ect, which is
built into this strategy. In this way, the phronetic researcher becomes a
part o
---

the project becoming simple action research.°
Phronetic researchers doing historical studies carry out much o
---

---

events. . . I try to answer this question without re
---
erring to the consciousness... the will... intention.’’’’ The researcher records what happened

(emphasis in original).'? Data, events, and phenomena are presented
together with their connections with other data, events, and phenomena.

Phronetic research is dialogical in the sense that it includes, and, i

The signi

 any given interpretation in a dialogue will depend
on the extent to which the interpretation’s validity claims are accepted,
and this acceptance typically occurs in competition with other validity
claims and other interpretations. The discourses in which results o
---

subordinated to the same conditions as any other dialogical discourse.
Some may

Phronetic social science

The result o
---
 phronetic research is a pragmatically governed interpretation o
---
 the studied practices. The interpretation does not require the
researcher to agree with the actors’ everyday understanding nor to discover some deep, inner meaning o
---
 the practices. Phronetic research is in
this way interpretive, but it is neither everyday nor deep hermeneutics.
Phronetic research is also not about, nor does it try to develop, theory or

universal method. Thus, phronetic research is an analytical project, but
not a theoretical or methodological one.
For this kind o
---
 research, practiced according to these heuristical
guidelines, I suggest the term “‘phronetic social science.’’ One task o
---

research practiced on the basis o
---
 the heuristics presented above would be
to provide concrete examples and detailed narratives o
---
 how power works
and with what consequences, and to suggest how power might be
changed and work with other consequences. Richard Rorty observes
about this that, “‘[i]n so 
---
ar as political situations become clear, they get
clari
---
ied by detailed stories about who’s doing what to whom.’”* Such
clari
---
ication is a principal concern 
---
or phronetic social science and provides the main link to praxis. Phronetic social science explores historic
circumstances and current practices to 
---
ind avenues to praxis. The task o
---

phronetic social science is to clari
---
y and deliberate about the problems
and risks we 
---
ace and to outline how things may be done di
---

---
erently, in 
---
ull
knowledge that we cannot 
---
ind ultimate answers to these questions or
even a single version o
---
 what the questions are.
In the 
---
ollowing chapter, I will illustrate how phronetic social science
may be carried out in practice.

Something happened

Modernity’s blind spot
Later, as a university lecturer, I 
---
ound that modernity
and democracy
have a “blind spot”’ in their re
---
lexivity regarding the real
relationship
between rationality and power. Ideals seem to block the view
to reality.
Modern democratic constitutions typically prescribe a separation
o
---
 rationality and power, much like the untenable separation o
---
 
---
act and value
in conventional social and political thinking. The ideal prescribes that

---
irst we must know about a problem, then we can decide about it. For
example, 
---
irst the civil servants in the administration investigate a policy
problem, then they in
---
orm their minister, who in
---
orms parliament, who
decides on the problem. Power is brought to bear on the problem only
a
---
ter we have made ourselves knowledgeable about it.
In reality, however, power o
---
ten ignores or designs knowledge at its
convenience. A consequence o
---
 the blind spot is that the real relationship
between rationality and power gets little attention both in conventional
constitution writing and in the research literature. There is a large gray
area between rationality and power, which is underinvestigated. This is
the area where the sort o
---

schools in Ribe County. The literature contains many studies o
---

---

---
ewer o
---

two.

I decided that as a scholar I would study this gray area. Accepting the
ideal that democratic decisions should be rational and in
---
ormed — an ideal
to which I, like other democrats, subscribe — should clearly not keep us

---
rom trying to understand how rationality and power really relate in real
decisions in real democracies. Firstly, Iwanted to study the phenomenon
that modern ideals o
---

---

a

with only weak guiding power and impact 
---
rom ideals to reality. Secondly, Iwanted to 
---

study these issues not only in theory, but also in practice. I 
---

---

---
or changing practice in a direction that would leave less scope 
---
or the
kind o
---

and elsewhere. Eventually, I decided to study how rationality and power
shape politics, administration, and planning in the town where I live and
work, Aalborg, Denmark.

Brie

---

and rationality produces power, their relationship is asymmetrical. Power
has a clear tendency to dominate rationality in the dynamic and overlapping relationship between the two. Paraphrasing Pascal, one could say
that power has a rationality that rationality does not know. Rationality, on
the other hand, does not have a power that power does not know. The
result is an unequal relationship between the two. Illegitimate rationalization and not rationality came to dominate the 
---
ate o
---
 the Aalborg

Project.'?

Project, would also have to agree with my analysis that

I have already mentioned Francis Bacon’s dictum that knowledge is
power. This dictum expresses the essence o
---
 Enlightenment thinking.
“Enlightenment is power,” and the more enlightenment — the more
rationality — the better. The Aalborg study shows that Bacon is right,
knowledge is power. But the study also shows that the inverse relation
between power and knowledge holds and that it is more important:
“‘Power is knowledge.” In this sense, the study stands Bacon on his head.
It shows how power de
---
ines what gets to count as knowledge. It shows,

---
urthermore, how power de
---
ines not only a certain conception o
---
 reality.
It is not just the social construction o
---

is also the 
---
act that power de
---
ines physical, economic, social, and environmental reality itsel
---
.
Modernity relies on rationality as the main means 
---
or making democracy work. But i
---
 the interrelations between rationality and power are
even remotely close to the asymmetrical relationship depicted in the
Aalborg case — which the tradition 
---
rom Thucydides, Machiavelli, and
Nietzsche tells us they are — then rationality is such a weak 
---
orm o
---
 power
that democracy built on rationality will be weak, too. The asymmetry
between rationality and power makes 
---
or a 
---
undamental weakness o
---

modernity and o
---
 modern democracy. The normative emphasis on rationality leaves the modern project as ignorant o
---
 how power works as the
guardians o
---
 the Aalborg Project and there
---
ore as open to being
dominated by power. Relying solely on rationality, there
---
ore, risks exacerbating the very problems modernity attempts to solve. Modernity and

power must be reconciled.
We need to assess the weakness o
---

---

the context-dependent nature o
---

---

thinkers like Machiavelli, Nietzsche, and Foucault. Constitution writing
and institutional re
---
orm based on discursive rationality a la Jurgen Habermas are shown in the Aalborg study to be inadequate. As I argued in
chapter seven, to enable democratic thinking and the public sphere to
make a real contribution to democratic action, we have to tie them back to
what they cannot accept in much o
---

with
con
---
lict, and partisanship. This, then, was the next step in my work
to exercise
the Aalborg Project: to become a partisan, to 
---
ace con
---

power.

How social science can matter again

What should be done?

In deciding on my praxis in relation to the Aalborg Project, I reasoned
that i
---
 the arrangements and outcomes o
---
 city politics and planning in
Aalborg were not publicly justi
---
iable, as my studies showed they were not,
then, perhaps, I could help change things 
---
or the better, even i
---
 only
modestly, by calling public attention to my results.'* In this way, my
studies would become part o
---
 the power relations they had uncovered.
I already mentioned that I chose to study the relationship between
rationality and power because this particular area o
---
 research was relatively unexplored, and because I hoped to help improve democracy’s re
---
lexivity on this point. By the time I began the Aalborg study, however, another,
more personal, motive had come along. I had now been a social scientist
long enough to be only too 
---
amiliar with the problem o
---
 the “‘so what
results’’ mentioned in chapter nine, research results that nobody cares
about. At one point I considered leaving the university altogether because
o
---
 this problem. Social science, without natural science’s link to technological development, and with only sporadic political relevance and support, seemed to me too isolated 
---
rom what is important in li
---
e to be worth
the e
---

---
ort o
---
 a li
---
etime’s work. I chose to stay, however, and tried to solve
my problem, in part by gradually developing and practicing the methodology described in this book. Phronetic social science would be an anti-

dote to the “‘so what’”’ problem.
Instead o
---
 natural science’s relevance to technological development, I
would conduct my research in ways that would make it relevant to
practical politics, administration, and planning. I tried to secure such
relevance by adopting two basic criteria. First, I would choose to work
with problems that are considered problems, not only in the academy, but
also in the rest o
---
 society. Second, I would deliberately and actively 
---
eed
the results o
---
 my research back into the political, administrative, and
social processes that I studied. For reasons I will return to below, at one

stage I called this way o
---
 working “‘research on the body.”’
Employing this approach in the Aalborg study has been a tall order and
has worked much better than I had reason to expect when I 
---
irst began to
develop it. One day, 
---
or example, I 
---
ound mysel
---

---

Radio (DR), participating in a direct, national broadcast about preliminary results 
---
rom my study. In order to get my 
---
eet wet and to gain
step-by-step experience with my new methodology, I had published
partial results o
---
 my research as I went along. ‘‘Trial balloons,” I called
them. This was the second time I sent up a balloon, and one o
---
the results
that drew public attention, nationally and locally, was the 
---
act that
whereas the Aalborg City Council had decided with the Aalborg Project

There were other incidents like this, and I mention them here to

emphasize

our things. Firstly, and most importantly, this way o

How social science can matter again

which is at the heart o

Social science that matters

Indeed he knows not how to know who knows not also how to un-know.
Richard Francis Burton

I
---
 we want to re-enchant and empower social science, and i
---
 we want to
recover social science 
---
rom its current role as loser in the Science Wars,

then we need to do three things. First, we must drop the 
---
ruitless e
---

---
orts to
emulate natural science’s success in producing cumulative and predictive
theory; this approach simply does not work in social science. Second, we
must take up problems that matter to the local, national, and global
communities in which we live, and we must do it in ways that matter; we

must 
---
ocus on issues o
---
 values and power like great social scientists have
advocated 
---
rom Aristotle and Machiavelli to Max Weber and Pierre
Bourdieu. Finally, we must e
---

---
ectively communicate the results o
---
 our
research to 
---
ellow citizens. I
---
 we do this, we may success
---
ully trans
---
orm
social science 
---
rom what is 
---
ast becoming a sterile academic activity,
which is undertaken mostly 
---
or its own sake and in increasing isolation

---
rom a society on which it has little e
---

---
ect and 
---
rom which it gets little
appreciation. We may trans
---
orm social science to an activity done in
public 
---
or the public, sometimes to clari
---
y, sometimes to intervene, sometimes to generate new perspectives, and always to serve as eyes and ears in
our ongoing e
---

---
orts at understanding the present and deliberating about
the 
---
uture. We may, in short, arrive at a social science that matters.

The development o
---
 social science seems to be constantly a
---

---
ected by
conjunctures where we attempt to clari
---
y to ourselves, and to others, what

we can and cannot do with this kind o
---
 science. By way o
---
 concluding this
book, I would like to develop two scenarios 
---
or clari
---
ication, the 
---
irst a
perpetuation o
---
 “‘science as usual,”’ the second, an emergent “‘phronetic
social science.” In the 
---
irst scenario, scientism, here understood as the
tendency to believe that science holds a reliable method o
---
 reaching the
truth about the nature o
---
 things, continues to dominate thinking in social
and political science.' Epistemic science and predictive theory are regarded as the pinnacle o
---
 scienti
---
ic endeavor in this scenario. The attempt
166

Social science that matters

167

to substitute theory and rules 
---
or phronesis will persist
. I
---
 this state o
---


a
---

---
airs continues, social and political research is likely to weake
n 
---
urther

as a scienti
---
ic activity. We are likely to continue to hear about
the “‘crisis o
---

social science.”’ The Science Wars will continue with the kind
o
---
 attacks
and counterattacks described in chapter one, and with social science on
the losing side. University administrators will continue to see sociology,
political science, and other social science departments as appropriate and
easy targets 
---
or cutbacks, when cutbacks are on the agenda. And all with
good reason: the social scientists’ era o
---
 glory in the 1960s and 1970s,
when they attempted to be architects in the development o
---
 the ‘‘Great
Society” and in other projects o
---
 the wel
---
are state, has long since passed.
These projects were built upon a natural science-inspired 
---
allacy, which
assumes a close association in social science between, on the one hand,

theoretical, basic science (episteme) and, on the other, practical, applied
science (techne).
The absence o
---
 this link between basic and applied social sciences does
not mean that these sciences do not continue to play a role as techne.
There remains a need 
---
or social sciences in the activities o
---
 the wel
---
are
society. These sciences help with registration, administration, control,
and redistribution o
---
 resources among various social groups. But in the

---
irst scenario such a role will consist o
---
 a “‘headless’’ 
---
orm o
---
 ad hoc
social engineering no longer given credence by a superstructure o
---

social-science theory. It will instead be dictated by a 
---
unctional meansrationality de
---
ined by the ruling relations o
---
 power. This kind o
---
 practical
social-science activity does not require advanced graduate and postgraduate specialized institutions o
---
 higher learning. The type o
---
 social
engineering we are speaking o
---
 here primarily demands midlevel generalists with an all-round Master’s or Bachelor’s degree: people who are

---
lexible enough to administer and execute the kinds o
---
 measures dictated
by whatever is considered instrumental at a given moment.
The second scenario replaces the view that the social sciences can be
practiced as episteme with their role as phronesis. In this scenario, the
purpose o
---
 social science is not to develop theory, but to contribute to
society’s practical rationality in elucidating where we are, where we want
to go, and what is desirable

according to diverse sets o
---
 values and

interests. The goal o
---
 the phronetic approach becomes one o
---
 contributing to society’s capacity 
---
or value-rational deliberation and action. The
contribution may be a combination o
---
 concrete empirical analyses =

practical philosophical considerations; ‘‘
---
ieldwork in philosophy,’’

as

.
Pierre Bourdieu calls it.
their role
A scenario o
---
 this kind will also involve the social sciences in
is, it will
as techne. However, when combined with the element o
---
 phrones

168

How social science can matter again

be a techne ‘“‘with a head on it,”’ that is, a techne governed by value-rational
deliberation. As mentioned earlier, it can be dangerous 
---
or individuals,

groups, and societies when their capacity 
---
or value-rational deliberation is
eroded. Today the erosion o
---
 such capacity seems to many to be rapidly
taking place and coincides with the growing incursion o
---
 a narrow meansrationality into social and political li
---
e. Simultaneously, there is a marked
need 
---
or discussion and reorientation o
---
 values and goals; 
---
or example, in
relation to environmental risks, work, health, international security, and
political stability. An evolution o
---
 the social sciences along the lines o
---
 this
second scenario, that is, as phronetic sciences, could help counter the

erosion o
---
 value-rationality and thereby help inhibit some o
---
 the destructive tendencies in society and in science.
Today, the dominant streak in social science continues to evolve along
the lines o
---
 the 
---
irst scenario, that o
---
 scientism. But scientism in social

science is sel
---
-de
---
eating because the reality o
---
 social science so evidently
does not live up to the ideals o
---
 scientism and natural science. There
---
ore it
is the second scenario, that o
---
 phronesis, which is more 
---
ertile, and worth

working 
---
or. This is the scenario I have tried to un
---
old in this book by
clari
---
ying what phronetic social science is and how it can be practiced.

Social1 Science Matter
"Making S
ae is~ Why social |inquiry 
---
ails and how it can succeed again

ae = : Making Social Science Matter presents an exciting new approach to the social and behavioral sciences. Instead o
---
 trying to emulate the natural sciences and create a kind o
---
 general
>. theory, Bent Flyvbjerg argues that the strength o
---
 the social sciences lies in their rich, re
---
lexive
analysis o
---
 values and power — so essential to the social and economic development o
---
 society. Moving beyond the purely analytic or technical, Flyvbjerg compares the theoretical study o
---

human activity with real-world situations and demonstrates how the social sciences can
become relevant again in the modern world. Power
---
ully argued, with clear methodological
guidelines and practical examples, Making Social Science Matter opens up a new 
---
uture 
---
or —
the social sciences, 
---
reed 
---
rom an inappropriate and misleading comparison with the natural
- sciences. The book’s empowering message will make it required reading 
---
or students and
academics across the social and behavioral sciences.
BENT FLYVBJERG is Pro
---
essor o
---
 Planning, Department o
---
 Development and Planning at
Aalborg University, Denmark. He is the author o
---
 numerous publications in twelve languages,
most recently Rationality and Power: Democracy in Practice .
